<span id="freeTextContainer4439343050961731212">I was warned before I started reading this that Thomas Hardy is a miserable bastard. Four chapters in and he was comparing winter mornings to dead babies so I can't disagree. <br/><br/>This wasn't a happy book but I did enjoy it. Hardy's prose are beautiful and the way he describes the forests and apple orchards really brings the setting to live. <br/><br/>I liked the characters for the most part. There are two typically "good" and "moral" characters and both get the saddest endings with one dying and the other doo</span>
  <span id="freeText4439343050961731212" style="display:none">I was warned before I started reading this that Thomas Hardy is a miserable bastard. Four chapters in and he was comparing winter mornings to dead babies so I can't disagree. <br /><br />This wasn't a happy book but I did enjoy it. Hardy's prose are beautiful and the way he describes the forests and apple orchards really brings the setting to live. <br /><br />I liked the characters for the most part. There are two typically "good" and "moral" characters and both get the saddest endings with one dying and the other doomed to a life of loneliness, shackled by their own faithfulness. <br /><br />There are no real "bad" characters. They're judgmental and selfish and they ruin their own lives and the lives of those around them by making the wrong choices. Even the main villains of the piece have fully human motivations and flaws. <br /><br />But yeah, Hardy is one miserable bastard and this is one miserable book.</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1169129057" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1169129057" />
    <a title="Joseph" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5095190-joseph"><img alt="Joseph" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1579812495p2/5095190.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1169129057?book_show_action=true">Jan 15, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Joseph" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Joseph" href="/user/show/5095190-joseph">Joseph</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/18848728-the-woodlanders">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5095190-joseph?shelf=literature">literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5095190-joseph?shelf=free-on-kindle">free-on-kindle</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1169129057" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7086705285075630852">If it's a Thomas Hardy novel, it's a tragedy. *sad face*<br/><br/>I was first exposed to Hardy in high school, being assigned <i>Jude the Obscure</i> for AP English. Entering the Navy, I was determined to continue to read, read, read, both -brow high and low, and eventually made my way through <i>Tess of the d'Urbervilles</i>, <i>Return of the Native</i>, <i>The Mayor of Casterbridge</i> and <i>Far from the Madding Crowd</i>. <i>The Woodlanders</i> was never on my radar, though, and it wasn't until I went on a "free for Kindle" purchasing binge, </span>
  <span id="freeText7086705285075630852" style="display:none">If it's a Thomas Hardy novel, it's a tragedy. *sad face*<br /><br />I was first exposed to Hardy in high school, being assigned <i>Jude the Obscure</i> for AP English. Entering the Navy, I was determined to continue to read, read, read, both -brow high and low, and eventually made my way through <i>Tess of the d'Urbervilles</i>, <i>Return of the Native</i>, <i>The Mayor of Casterbridge</i> and <i>Far from the Madding Crowd</i>. <i>The Woodlanders</i> was never on my radar, though, and it wasn't until I went on a "free for Kindle" purchasing binge, was I reminded of Hardy and the novels of his that I had never read.<br /><br /><i>The Woodlanders</i> follows the tale of the folk of Hintock, Great and Little, and more specifically Grace Melbury, daughter of a successful businessman who has endeavored for her to be educated far beyond the status of most country girls of the day. Although her father has promised her to the son of a man the father once wronged, ambition soon rules the day, and the daughter is made to seek after greener pastures. What follows is heartache, betrayal, indignation, sorrow, death, and then hope. And then sorrow again. Seriously, in the last couple of chapters I'm like, "OMG, everyone is going to end up dead in a pool of blood!" Thankfully, it didn't turn out that way, but Hardy manages to twist the knife of tragedy once more into the one character who was always honest and noble. *tut* Damn you, Thomas Hardy!!<br /><br />Hardy's prose is lyrical, because he was also a poet. Those novels of his I read when younger were Penguin Classics editions, the ones with the black banner across the top and old paintings as the cover art. They were annotated, thankfully, because when reading Hardy one needs a dictionary of 19th century antiquated words and a guide to the various laws which governed inheritances. My trusty Kindle helped (some) with the words, but there was one legal matter that I still can't quite grasp (another example of our noble and honest hero being betrayed by Hardy).<br /><br />I'm only giving this four stars instead of the five I would probably give <i>Jude</i> and <i>Tess</i> because I feel the plot is not as rich and deep as theirs. Hardy also does not give as thorough a psychological examination of the characters as I feel he did in his other works, and finally, because <i>The Woodlanders</i>, as a title, does not match the panache of <i>Tess of the d'Urbervilles</i>, <i>Jude the Obscure</i>, and <i>Far from the Madding Crowd</i>. It sounds more like a story about the meeting of tiny little elven folk who live betwixt here and there in the shadows of the forest.<br /><br />This was a lovely book to read, however, and I had a hard time putting it down. Hardy provides a beautiful portrait of a lady...oops, wrong author...examination, rather, of a time when social mores and stations were rigidly enforced, as well as bringing to life the lovely Wessex countryside. In all seriousness, Hardy was a master at this type of novel, and he truly deserves his reputation and place as one of the all time greatest writers of the English language. </span>
  <a data-text-id="7086705285075630852" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1512025729" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1512025729" />
    <a title="Ann Litz" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/44312445-ann-litz"><img alt="Ann Litz" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1435456850p2/44312445.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1512025729?book_show_action=true">Jan 16, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ann Litz" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ann Litz" href="/user/show/44312445-ann-litz">Ann Litz</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1512025729" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14812271174588824861">One of the joys of reading an early book by an author famous for later works is recognizing the intimations of the greater novels in the lesser. In this sense <i>Woodlanders</i> is a lavishly illustrated seed-catalog of Hardy classics.<br/><br/>The novel begins with one of the most desolate passages in literature, and one that succintly summarizes the novel’s theme of happiness just out of reach:<br/><br/>“The physiognomy of a deserted highway expresses solitude to a degree that is not reached by mere dales or downs, and </span>
  <span id="freeText14812271174588824861" style="display:none">One of the joys of reading an early book by an author famous for later works is recognizing the intimations of the greater novels in the lesser. In this sense <i>Woodlanders</i> is a lavishly illustrated seed-catalog of Hardy classics.<br /><br />The novel begins with one of the most desolate passages in literature, and one that succintly summarizes the novel’s theme of happiness just out of reach:<br /><br />“The physiognomy of a deserted highway expresses solitude to a degree that is not reached by mere dales or downs, and bespeaks a tomb-like stillness more emphatic than that of glades and pools. The contrast of what is with what might be probably accounts for this.”<br /><br />Deep and foreshadowing imagery, especially in the natural world, persists throughout the novel -- such as a particular winter day that emerges “like a dead-born child.”<br /><br />Hardy also associates different characters with different seasons -- Dr. Fitzpiers, for example, with false spring, and Giles with robust autumn. The references to Norse mythology are a welcome respite from Hardy’s usual Greco-Roman archetypes.<br /><br />But the real pleasure (if a reader can call it that) is the scattering of Hardy-esque touchstones:<br /><br />* Parental pressure for a woman to marry “up” no matter what, even when the woman herself insists it would be a mistake (<i>Tess of the D’Urbervilles</i>)<br /><br />* Misunderstandings and missed messages (just about every Hardy novel)<br /><br />*At least one character “always doomed to sacrifice desire to obligation” (<i>Return of the Native,</i> <i>Far from the Madding Crowd</i>)<br /><br />* Idealization that leads to dangerous infatuation with an unknown person (<i>Native</i>)<br /><br />* Marrying the “wrong” person (every Hardy novel ever)<br /><br />* Female characters bearing the consequences of the male characters’ follies; “the woman pays” (<i>Tess</i>)<br /><br />* Relishment of self-punishment, “cultivating, as under glass, strange and mournful pleasures” (<i>Jude</i>)<br /><br />* Marriage itself being linked imagistically with gins and traps, particularly the sound of a desperate trapped rabbit (<i>Jude</i>)<br /><br />* Two people who are actually halves of a whole (<i>Jude</i>)<br /><br />In itself, <i>Woodlanders</i> is a fine novel, even if at times it lapses into convenient coincidence more often even than most Dickens novels. Innocent characters are punished while at least one villain seems to be outright rewarded, as if Hardy’s reaction to Darwin is not that the best of a species survive but the worst.<br /><br /><i>Woodlanders</i> is a proper introduction to the harsh physical and psychological world of Thomas Hardy’s Wessex.<br /><br />For someone who’s already a fan of the master of despair, <i>Woodlanders</i> is the sketchbook of what would become Hardy’s brilliant masterpieces. </span>
  <a data-text-id="14812271174588824861" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_126391460" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/126391460" />
    <a title="Laura" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3542525-laura"><img alt="Laura" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1270482159p2/3542525.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/126391460?book_show_action=true">Oct 15, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Laura" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Laura" href="/user/show/3542525-laura">Laura</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140620900" href="/book/show/898260.The_Woodlanders">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer126391460" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15153300292675259220">I have a thing for Thomas Hardy. Maybe my husband should be worried.<br/>Normally mothers have to take the blame for everything, but this novel is about a father and his love for his daughter. It compels him to give her a good education at a finishing school: when the girl returns to her hometown there is nowhere for her to show her accomplishments or use her skills. It also makes her grow apart from her childhood sweetheart, the man her dad would like her to marry. This is the paradox at the heart o</span>
  <span id="freeText15153300292675259220" style="display:none">I have a thing for Thomas Hardy. Maybe my husband should be worried.<br />Normally mothers have to take the blame for everything, but this novel is about a father and his love for his daughter. It compels him to give her a good education at a finishing school: when the girl returns to her hometown there is nowhere for her to show her accomplishments or use her skills. It also makes her grow apart from her childhood sweetheart, the man her dad would like her to marry. This is the paradox at the heart of the novel.<br />As a result, this girl, Grace, is going to find herself torn apart between Giles, who is deeply and constantly in love with her, and Fitzpiers, the new doctor in the area. It is very intriguing how her dad tries to engineer first a relationship with Giles and then with Fitzpiers, and then back to Giles and so forth, so Grace does not really know her own feelings and falls into a relationship almost by default. <br />This is the case with Grace and Fitzpiers. They fancy themselves in love, they will themselves to fall in love. By contrast, Giles and Marty South, victims of unrequited love, are steady and unmoved in their feelings regardless of circumstances. I wonder whether Thomas Hardy is suggesting that civilization and education complicate things a bit too much.<br />The structure of this book is awesome. It starts off and finishes with the two single appearances of the same character, the barber. Also, like in "A Pair of Blue Eyes", Hardy manages to get the protagonist out of her clothes at a key point in the story. The part where Giles behaves so gentlemanly towards Grace as to risk his own life as a result, seems a bit farcical to me and difficult to believe, but then I am a twenty-first century reader. I really liked how at the end of the book Grace's dad and his friends, coming back from a search party, judge how the situation between Grace and Fitzpiers is going to go from then, and retell various stories about marriage, Hardy's leit-motiv.<br />I have never been a great fan of the countryside, but I appreciate the beauty of English villages and this is a side of Hardy that I really enjoy too.<br />How I wish he were a member of goodreads and could clik on the "I like" button after reading my review.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="15153300292675259220" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_928850666" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/928850666" />
    <a title="K." class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/861629-k"><img alt="K." src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1342794238p2/861629.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/928850666?book_show_action=true">May 05, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="K." class="user" itemprop="url" name="K." href="/user/show/861629-k">K.</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/18848728-the-woodlanders">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/861629-k?shelf=didn-t-finish-various-reasons">didn-t-finish-various-reasons</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer928850666" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13603235909932381914">Thought I'd try out Mr. Hardy again, it's been a while. At times, he is delightful. At times he is melodramatic and morose. Sometimes he's in-between. I love him delightful, I can appreciate him in the middle, I just nearly gag when he's wallowing in morbidity . Sadly, this book, at least the 36% I read of it, anyway, fits in the latter category. I knew that could be the case and went into this book with eyes wide open. I'm not averse to the characters or story so far, as much as apathetic. But,</span>
  <span id="freeText13603235909932381914" style="display:none">Thought I'd try out Mr. Hardy again, it's been a while. At times, he is delightful. At times he is melodramatic and morose. Sometimes he's in-between. I love him delightful, I can appreciate him in the middle, I just nearly gag when he's wallowing in morbidity . Sadly, this book, at least the 36% I read of it, anyway, fits in the latter category. I knew that could be the case and went into this book with eyes wide open. I'm not averse to the characters or story so far, as much as apathetic. But, you know, it's hard to love a book when there are lines like this in it: <br /><br /><b>"There was now a distinct manifestation of morning in the air, and presently the bleared white visage of a sunless winter day emerged like a dead-born child." </b><br /><br />Okay, that is quite possibly the most ghastly way to describe a blah-ish winter morning I have ever encountered. I mean, <i>really</i>. Could anything be more repellent? I suppose so, but one must admit (unless you're a member of the "Jude the Obscure" is the world's greatest book crowd) it's pretty bad. <br /><br />I don't mean to suggest that the whole tone of the book follows suit, or that it's a bad book. How can I, when I'm leaving it two-thirds unfinished? But it's distasteful enough so I find myself overwhelmingly uninterested in finishing it, at the present anyhow. <br /><br />I also don't mean to suggest that extremely disgruntled, depressed, oppressed, despairing people shouldn't write their feelings out on paper...I just don't care to spend time with them right now. I <i>do</i> mean, if you're a real person, a friend of mine, on my couch next to me, with a big problem, I'll keep you company and all that, but I'm not going in search of Jude and Sue. <br /><br />And just so you know, I've read enough Hardy to judge for myself (in case you think I'm just a low-brow loser). <br /><br />I'm done being snarky now. It's fun on occasion. </span>
  <a data-text-id="13603235909932381914" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<div id="review_230959415" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/230959415" />
    <a title="Jayaprakash Satyamurthy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1497559-jayaprakash-satyamurthy"><img alt="Jayaprakash Satyamurthy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1517076488p2/1497559.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/230959415?book_show_action=true">Nov 04, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jayaprakash Satyamurthy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jayaprakash Satyamurthy" href="/user/show/1497559-jayaprakash-satyamurthy">Jayaprakash Satyamurthy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140431452" href="/book/show/898256.The_Woodlanders">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer230959415" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16824503986112487943">Throughout this novel I was taken by the way Hardy visualises scenes either through subjective viewpoints, showing us what specific characters see, or choose to see, or from the eye of the omniscient observer, the author. Some of my favorite novelists - Graham Greene is another example - excel in the art of sequencing, chosing the most telling scene to establish theme, character and setting and advance plot. Hardy displays a similar knack here, with each episode bearing its own strength and unit</span>
  <span id="freeText16824503986112487943" style="display:none">Throughout this novel I was taken by the way Hardy visualises scenes either through subjective viewpoints, showing us what specific characters see, or choose to see, or from the eye of the omniscient observer, the author. Some of my favorite novelists - Graham Greene is another example - excel in the art of sequencing, chosing the most telling scene to establish theme, character and setting and advance plot. Hardy displays a similar knack here, with each episode bearing its own strength and unity while contributing inexorably to the larger narrative. Setting and story are inextricably woven together, with the natural surroundings of the village of Little Hintock playing their own role as a character in this story. <br /><br />Hardy is subtle and incisive in his characterisation, although he does give way to melodrama in certain crucial scenes - scenes that seem to be able to bear the burden of so much emotion, however. His unsentimental take on social divides and institutions gives the story of cross-connections in the romantic affairs of a handful of people a timelessness and freshness that more conventional tales from this era lack. <br /><br />Most of all, I was drawn in by the quality of Hardy's prose; penetrating, beguiling, wryly aware of human foibles, yet never sententious, always ready with a telling detail and an apt allusion. They don't write them like this anymore. </span>
  <a data-text-id="16824503986112487943" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('mdyENqAkmj3LwZN51OwLXeKWWbHibWL3vJ5SHROII1mlEa/Bd2HZ+gMUX5KR9HUOfegIA+/wViJeeOimBDJQvw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('hbbPPFYoJMue0UCYRHsjXAsh4JK9E9vqVBJCaiCqMrO5e+TLgW1nDFYEjHMBY10PlF+xILCO7z+29PjRNxBBVQ==')})
  }
//]]>

One is the shy Caroline Helstone, who is trapped in the oppressive atmosphere of a Yorkshire rectory and whose bare life symbolizes the plight of single women in the nineteenth century. The other is the vivacious Shirley Keeldar, who inherits a local estate and whose wealth liberates her from convention.A work that combines social commentary with the more private preoccupations of Jane Eyre, Shirley demonstrates the full range of Brontë\'s literary talent. &quot;Shirley is a revolutionary novel,&quot; wrote Brontë biographer Lyndall Gordon. &quot;Shirley follows Jane Eyre as a new exemplar but so much a forerunner of the feminist of the later twentieth century that it is hard to believe in her actual existence in 1811-12. She is a theoretic possibility: what a woman might be if she combined independence and means of her own with intellect. Charlotte Brontë imagined a new form of power, equal to that of men, in a confident young woman [whose] extraordinary freedom has accustomed her to think for herself....Shirley [is] Brontë\'s most       var newTip = new Tip($('bookCover_835508'), "\n\n  <h2><a class=\"readable bookTitle\" href=\"https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/835508.Romola?from_choice=false&amp;from_home_module=false\">Romola<\/a><\/h2>\n\n      <div>\n        by <a class=\"authorName\" href=\"/author/show/173.George_Eliot?a=9&amp;origin=related_works\">George Eliot<\/a>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p6\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p0\"><\/span><\/span> 3.75 avg rating &mdash; 3,959 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published 1863\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer6024055427074680232\">One of George Eliot\'s most ambitious and imaginative novels, Romola is set in Renaissance Florence during the turbulent years following the expulsion of the powerful Medici family during which the zealous religious reformer Savonarola rose to control<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText6024055427074680232\" style=\"display:none\">One of George Eliot\'s most ambitious and imaginative novels, Romola is set in Renaissance Florence during the turbulent years following the expulsion of the powerful Medici family during which the zealous religious reformer Savonarola rose to control the city.       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/15905.Thomas_Hardy">About Thomas Hardy</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/15905.Thomas_Hardy"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1429946281p3/15905.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/15905.Thomas_Hardy">Thomas Hardy
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
4,732
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer13519611698893796522"><b>Thomas Hardy</b>, OM, was an English author of the naturalist movement, although in several poems he displays elements of the previous romantic and enlightenment periods of literature, such as his fascination with the supernatural. He regarded himself primarily as a poet and composed novels mainly for financial gain. The bulk of his work, set mainly in the semi-fictional land of Wessex, delineates cha</span>
  <span id="freeText13519611698893796522" style="display:none"><b>Thomas Hardy</b>, OM, was an English author of the naturalist movement, although in several poems he displays elements of the previous romantic and enlightenment periods of literature, such as his fascination with the supernatural. He regarded himself primarily as a poet and composed novels mainly for financial gain. The bulk of his work, set mainly in the semi-fictional land of Wessex, delineates characters struggling against their passions and circumstances. Hardy's poetry, first published in his 50s, has come to be as well regarded as his novels, especially after The Movement of the 1950s and 1960s. The term <i>
  <b>cliffhanger</b>
</i> is considered to have originated with Thomas Hardy's serial novel <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/110077.A_Pair_of_Blue_Eyes" title="A Pair of Blue Eyes" rel="nofollow noopener">A Pair of Blue Eyes</a> in 1873. In the novel, Hardy chose to leave one of his protagonists, Knight, literally hanging off a cliff staring into the stony eyes of a trilobite embedded in the rock that has been dead for millions of years. This became the archetypal — and literal — cliff-hanger of Victorian prose.<br /><br /><i>Excerpted from <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thomas_Hardy" rel="nofollow noopener">Wikipedia</a>.</i></span>
  <a data-text-id="13519611698893796522" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>

<div class="clear"></div></div></div><div class="bigBoxBottom"></div></div>

      <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/2502604-the-woodlanders">Quotes from The Woodlanders</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">

Only days and days? O, the heart of a man! Days and days!'<br />'But, my dear madam, I had not known you more than a day or two. It was not a full-blown love - it was the merest bud - red, fresh, vivid, but small. It was a colossal passion in embryo. It never returned.&rdquo;</span>
